	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Timeline of scientific discoveries</span></h1>

<p>
The timeline below shows the date of publication of possible major <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> breakthroughs, theories and discoveries, along with the discoverer.  This article discounts mere speculation as discovery, although imperfect reasoned arguments, arguments based on elegance/simplicity, and numerically/experimentally verified conjectures qualify (as otherwise no scientific discovery before the late 19th century would count). The timeline begins at the Bronze Age, as it is difficult to give even estimates for the timing of events prior to this, such as of the discovery of counting, natural numbers and arithmetic.
</p><p>To avoid overlap with <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_historic_inventions" title="Timeline of historic inventions">timeline of historic inventions</a>, the timeline does not list examples of documentation for manufactured substances and devices unless they reveal a more fundamental leap in the theoretical ideas in a field.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-60"><a href="#Notation_and_conventions_3"><span class="tocnumber">5.1.7</span> <span class="toctext">Notation and conventions</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>

<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Bronze Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many early innovations of the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> were prompted by the increase in <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>, and this also applies to the scientific advances of this period. For context, the major civilizations of this period are Egypt, Mesopotamia, and the Indus Valley, with Greece rising in importance towards the end of the third millennium BC. It is to be noted that the Indus Valley script remains undeciphered and there are very little surviving fragments of its writing, thus any inference about scientific discoveries in the region must be made based only on archaeological digs.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Numbers.2C_measurement_and_arithmetic"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers,_measurement_and_arithmetic">Numbers, measurement and arithmetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Numbers, measurement and arithmetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Around 3000 BC: <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" class="mw-redirect" title="Units of measurement">Units of measurement</a> are developed in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> as well as the major Bronze Age civilisations: <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Indus Valley">Indus Valley</a>. The Indus Valley may have been the major innovator on this, as the first measurement devices (rulers, protractors, weighing scales) were invented in <a href="/wiki/Lothal" title="Lothal">Lothal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitelaw14_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitelaw14-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rao401_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rao401-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jstor44_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor44-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1800 BC: Fractions were first studied by the Egyptians in their study of <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_fractions" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian fractions">Egyptian fractions</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>2100 BC: The concept of <a href="/wiki/Area" title="Area">area</a> is first recognised in Babylonian clay tablets,<sup id="cite_ref-Friberg2009_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friberg2009-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and 3-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> is discussed in an <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Mathematical_Papyrus" title="Moscow Mathematical Papyrus">Egyptian papyrus</a>. This begins the study of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>.</li>
<li>Early 2nd millennium BC: Similar triangles and side-ratios are studied in Egypt (e.g. in the <a href="/wiki/Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus" title="Rhind Mathematical Papyrus">Rhind Mathematical Papyrus</a>, a copy of an older <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a> text) for the construction of pyramids, paving the way for the field of <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maor-20_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maor-20-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra">Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>2100 BC: <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Quadratic equations">Quadratic equations</a>, in the form of problems relating the areas and sides of rectangles, are solved by Babylonians.<sup id="cite_ref-Friberg2009_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friberg2009-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_discrete_mathematics">Number theory and discrete mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Number theory and discrete mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>2000 BC: Pythagorean triples are first discussed in Babylon and Egypt, and appear on later manuscripts such as the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Papyrus_6619" title="Berlin Papyrus 6619">Berlin Papyrus 6619</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_mathematics_and_algorithms">Numerical mathematics and algorithms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Numerical mathematics and algorithms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>2000 BC: Multiplication tables in Babylon.<sup id="cite_ref-Qiu_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qiu-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1800 BC – 1600 BC: A numerical approximation for the square root of two, accurate to 6 decimal places, is recorded on <a href="/wiki/YBC_7289" title="YBC 7289">YBC 7289</a>, a Babylonian clay tablet believed to belong to a student.<sup id="cite_ref-bs_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bs-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>19th to 17th century BCE: A Babylonian tablet uses <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">25</span>&#8260;<span class="den">8</span></span> = 3.125 as an approximation for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, which has an error of 0.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Early 2nd millennium BCE: The <a href="/wiki/Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus" title="Rhind Mathematical Papyrus">Rhind Mathematical Papyrus</a> (a copy of an older <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a> text) contains the first documented instance of inscribing a polygon (in this case, an octagon) into a circle to estimate the value of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rossi_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rossi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Notation_and_conventions">Notation and conventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Notation and conventions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>3000 BC: The first deciphered numeral system is that of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_numerals" title="Egyptian numerals">Egyptian numerals</a>, a sign-value system (as opposed to a place-value system).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2000 BC: Primitive positional notation for numerals is seen in the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_cuneiform_numerals" title="Babylonian cuneiform numerals">Babylonian cuneiform numerals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chrisomalis2_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chrisomalis2-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> However, the lack of clarity around the notion of <a href="/wiki/Zero" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero">zero</a> made their system highly ambiguous (e.g. <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7004132000000000000♠"></span>13<span style="margin-left:.25em;">200</span></span> would be written the same as <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002132000000000000♠"></span>132</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-lmnz_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lmnz-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Early 2nd millennium BC: The periodicity of planetary phenomenon<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> is recognised by Babylonian astronomers.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_and_anatomy">Biology and anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biology and anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Early 2nd millennium BC: Ancient Egyptians study anatomy, as recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Smith_Papyrus" title="Edwin Smith Papyrus">Edwin Smith Papyrus</a>. They identified the heart and its vessels, liver, spleen, kidneys, hypothalamus, uterus, and bladder, and correctly identified that blood vessels emanated from the heart (however, they also believed that tears, urine, and semen, but not saliva and sweat, originated in the heart, see <a href="/wiki/Cardiocentric_hypothesis" title="Cardiocentric hypothesis">Cardiocentric hypothesis</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Porter1999_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_Age">Iron Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Iron Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_2">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry_2">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 700 BC: The Pythagoras theorem is discovered by <a href="/wiki/Baudhayana" class="mw-redirect" title="Baudhayana">Baudhayana</a> in the Hindu <a href="/wiki/Shulba_Sutras" title="Shulba Sutras">Shulba Sutras</a> in Upanishadic India.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> However, Indian mathematics, especially North Indian mathematics, generally did not have a tradition of communicating proofs, and it is not fully certain that Baudhayana or <a href="/wiki/Apastamba" class="mw-redirect" title="Apastamba">Apastamba</a> knew of a proof.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_discrete_mathematics_2">Number theory and discrete mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Number theory and discrete mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 700 BC: <a href="/wiki/Pell%27s_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Pell&#39;s equations">Pell's equations</a> are first studied by Baudhayana in India, the first diophantine equations known to be studied.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry_3">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 600 BC: <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">Thales of Miletus</a> discovers <a href="/wiki/Thales%27s_theorem" title="Thales&#39;s theorem">Thales's theorem</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_and_anatomy_2">Biology and anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Biology and anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>600 BC – 200 BC: The <a href="/wiki/Sushruta_Samhita" title="Sushruta Samhita">Sushruta Samhita</a> shows an understanding of musculoskeletal structure (including joints, ligaments and muscles and their functions) (3.V).<sup id="cite_ref-bhisha_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhisha-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> It refers to the cardiovascular system as a closed circuit.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In (3.IX) it identifies the existence of nerves.<sup id="cite_ref-bhisha_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhisha-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_science">Social science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Social science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistics">Linguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Linguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 700 BC: <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">Grammar</a> is first studied in India (note that Sanskrit <a href="/wiki/Vy%C4%81kara%E1%B9%87a" title="Vyākaraṇa">Vyākaraṇa</a> predates <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a>).</li></ul>
<h2><span id="500_BC_.E2.80.93_1_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="500_BC_–_1_BC">500 BC – 1 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 500 BC – 1 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Greeks make numerous advances in mathematics and astronomy through the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic</a> periods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_3">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic_and_proof">Logic and proof</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Logic and proof">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>4th century BC: Greek philosophers study the properties of logical <a href="/wiki/Negation" title="Negation">negation</a>.</li>
<li>4th century BC: The first true formal system is constructed by <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a> in his Sanskrit grammar.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 300 BC: Greek mathematician <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid&#39;s Elements"><i>Elements</i></a> describes a primitive form of formal proof and axiomatic systems. However, modern mathematicians generally believe that his axioms were highly incomplete, and that his definitions were not really used in his proofs.</li></ul>
<h4><span id="Numbers.2C_measurement_and_arithmetic_2"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers,_measurement_and_arithmetic_2">Numbers, measurement and arithmetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Numbers, measurement and arithmetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus of Cnidus</a> states the <a href="/wiki/Archimedean_property" title="Archimedean property">Archimedean property</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Knopp1951_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knopp1951-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>4th-3rd century BC: In Mauryan India, The Jain mathematical text <a href="/w/index.php?title=Surya_Prajnapati&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Surya Prajnapati (page does not exist)">Surya Prajnapati</a> draws a distinction between countable and uncountable infinities.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Pingala" title="Pingala">Pingala</a> in Mauryan India studies <a href="/wiki/Binary_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary numbers">binary numbers</a>, making him the first to study the <a href="/wiki/Radix" title="Radix">radix</a> (numerical base) in history.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra_2">Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>5th century BC: Possible date of the discovery of the triangular numbers (i.e. the sum of consecutive integers), by the Pythagoreans.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 300 BC: Finite geometric progressions are studied by Euclid in Ptolemaic Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>3rd century BC: Archimedes relates problems in geometric series to those in arithmetic series, foreshadowing the <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>190 BC: <a href="/wiki/Magic_squares" class="mw-redirect" title="Magic squares">Magic squares</a> appear in China. The theory of magic squares can be considered the first example of a <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector space</a>.</li>
<li>165-142 BC: <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Cang" title="Zhang Cang">Zhang Cang</a> in Northern China is credited with the development of Gaussian elimination.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_discrete_mathematics_3">Number theory and discrete mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Number theory and discrete mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 500 BC: <a href="/wiki/Hippasus" title="Hippasus">Hippasus</a>, a Pythagorean, discovers irrational numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(mathematician)" title="Theaetetus (mathematician)">Thaetetus</a> shows that square roots are either integer or irrational.</li>
<li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(mathematician)" title="Theaetetus (mathematician)">Thaetetus</a> enumerates the Platonic solids, an early work in graph theory.</li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Pingala" title="Pingala">Pingala</a> in Mauryan India describes the Fibonacci sequence.<sup id="cite_ref-HistoriaMathematica_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistoriaMathematica-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-knuth-v1_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knuth-v1-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 300 BC: Euclid proves the infinitude of primes.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 300 BC: Euclid proves the Fundamental Theorem of Arithmetic.</li>
<li>c. 300 BC: Euclid discovers the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_algorithm" title="Euclidean algorithm">Euclidean algorithm</a>.</li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Pingala" title="Pingala">Pingala</a> in Mauryan India discovers the binomial coefficients in a combinatorial context and the additive formula for generating them <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle {\tbinom {n}{r}}={\tbinom {n-1}{r}}+{\tbinom {n-1}{r-1}}}">

<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> discovers the <a href="/wiki/Sieve_of_Eratosthenes" title="Sieve of Eratosthenes">Sieve of Eratosthenes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nicomachus_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nicomachus-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry_4">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>5th century BC: The Greeks start experimenting with straightedge-and-compass constructions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bold_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bold-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Menaechmus" title="Menaechmus">Menaechmus</a> discovers conic sections.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Menaechmus" title="Menaechmus">Menaechmus</a> develops co-ordinate geometry.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 300 BC: Euclid publishes the <i>Elements</i>, a compendium on classical Euclidean geometry, including: elementary theorems on circles, definitions of the centers of a triangle, the tangent-secant theorem, the law of sines and the law of cosines.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyer_Early_Trigonometry_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer_Early_Trigonometry-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> derives a formula for the volume of a sphere in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Method_of_Mechanical_Theorems" title="The Method of Mechanical Theorems">The Method of Mechanical Theorems</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> calculates areas and volumes relating to conic sections, such as the area bounded between a parabola and a chord, and various volumes of revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> discovers the sum/difference identity for trigonometric functions in the form of the "Theorem of Broken Chords".<sup id="cite_ref-Boyer_Early_Trigonometry_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer_Early_Trigonometry-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 200 BC: <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius of Perga</a> discovers <a href="/wiki/Apollonius%27s_theorem" title="Apollonius&#39;s theorem">Apollonius's theorem</a>.</li>
<li>c. 200 BC: <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius of Perga</a> assigns equations to curves.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Late 5th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Antiphon_(person)" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiphon (person)">Antiphon</a> discovers the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion" title="Method of exhaustion">method of exhaustion</a>, foreshadowing the concept of a limit.</li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> makes use of infinitesimals.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> further develops the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion" title="Method of exhaustion">method of exhaustion</a> into an early description of <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">integration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>3rd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> calculates tangents to non-trigonometric curves.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_mathematics_and_algorithms_2">Numerical mathematics and algorithms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Numerical mathematics and algorithms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>3rd century BC: Archimedes uses the method of exhaustion to construct a strict inequality bounding the value of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> within an interval of 0.002.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy_2">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>5th century BC: The earliest documented mention of a spherical Earth comes from the Greeks in the 5th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-dicks1_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dicks1-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>  It is known that the Indians modeled the Earth as spherical by 300 BC<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>500 BC: <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a> identifies moonlight as reflected sunlight.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>260 BC: <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> proposes a basic heliocentric model of the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 200 BC: Apollonius of Perga develops <a href="/wiki/Epicycles" class="mw-redirect" title="Epicycles">epicycles</a>. While an incorrect model, it was a precursor to the development of <a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier series</a>.</li>
<li>2nd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Hipparchos" class="mw-redirect" title="Hipparchos">Hipparchos</a> discovers the apsidal precession of the Moon's orbit.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2nd century BC: <a href="/wiki/Hipparchos" class="mw-redirect" title="Hipparchos">Hipparchos</a> discovers <a href="/wiki/Axial_precession" title="Axial precession">Axial precession</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanics">Mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>3rd century BC: Archimedes develops the field of statics, introducing notions such as the center of gravity, mechanical equilibrium, the study of levers, and hydrostatics.</li>
<li>350-50 BC: Clay tablets from (possibly Hellenistic-era) Babylon describe the mean speed theorem.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics">Optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Mozi_(book)" title="Mozi (book)">Mozi</a> in China gives a description of the <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a> phenomenon.</li>
<li>c. 300 BC: <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Optics" title="Euclid&#39;s Optics">Euclid's <i>Optics</i></a> introduces the field of geometric optics, making basic considerations on the sizes of images.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_physics">Thermal physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Thermal physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>460 BC: Empedocles describes thermal expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-valleriani_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valleriani-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Matter">Matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul><li>600BC: <a href="/wiki/Ka%E1%B9%87%C4%81da_(philosopher)" title="Kaṇāda (philosopher)">Maharshi Kanada</a> gives <b>the ideal of the smallest units of matter</b>. According to him, matter consisted of indestructible minutes particles called <i>paramanus</i>, which are now called as atoms.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_and_anatomy_3">Biology and anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Biology and anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>4th century BC: Around the time of Aristotle, a more empirically founded system of anatomy is established, based on animal dissection. In particular, <a href="/wiki/Praxagoras" title="Praxagoras">Praxagoras</a> makes the distinction between arteries and veins.</li>
<li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> differentiates between <a href="/wiki/Near-sightedness#History" class="mw-redirect" title="Near-sightedness">near-sighted</a> and far-sightedness.<sup id="cite_ref-myopia_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-myopia-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Graeco-Roman physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> would later use the term "myopia" for near-sightedness.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_science_2">Social science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Social science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg/220px-Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg/330px-Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg/440px-Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5216" data-file-height="3408" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birch_bark_MS_from_Kashmir_of_the_Rupavatra_Wellcome_L0032691.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a>'s <a href="/wiki/A%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%AD%C4%81dhy%C4%81y%C4%AB" title="Aṣṭādhyāyī">Aṣṭādhyāyī</a>, an early Indian grammatical treatise that constructs a formal system for the purpose of describing Sanskrit grammar.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Late 4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/Chanakya" title="Chanakya">Chanakya</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Kautilya" class="mw-redirect" title="Kautilya">Kautilya</a>) establishes the field of economics with the <a href="/wiki/Arthashastra" title="Arthashastra">Arthashastra</a> (literally "Science of wealth"), a prescriptive treatise on economics and statecraft for Mauryan India.<sup id="cite_ref-Mabbett_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mabbett-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistics_2">Linguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Linguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>4th century BC: <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a> develops a full-fledged formal grammar (for Sanskrit).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_and_geospatial_measurements">Astronomical and geospatial measurements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Astronomical and geospatial measurements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>3rd century BC: Eratosthenes measures the circumference of the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2nd century BC: Hipparchos measures the sizes of and distances to the moon and sun.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span id="1_AD_.E2.80.93_500_AD"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1_AD_–_500_AD">1 AD – 500 AD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: 1 AD – 500 AD">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mathematics and astronomy flourish during the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_India" title="Golden Age of India">Golden Age of India</a> (4th to 6th centuries AD) under the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta Empire</a>. Meanwhile, Greece and its colonies have entered the <a href="/wiki/Greece_in_the_Roman_era" title="Greece in the Roman era">Roman period</a> in the last few decades of the preceding millennium, and Greek science is negatively impacted by the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">Fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> and the economic decline that follows.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_4">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Numbers.2C_measurement_and_arithmetic_3"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers,_measurement_and_arithmetic_3">Numbers, measurement and arithmetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Numbers, measurement and arithmetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:P._Lund,_Inv._35a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Fragment of papyrus with clear Greek script, lower-right corner suggests a tiny zero with a double-headed arrow shape above it" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg/220px-P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg/330px-P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg/440px-P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:P._Lund,_Inv._35a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of the early Greek symbol for zero (lower right corner) from a 2nd-century papyrus</div></div></div>
<ul><li>210 AD: <a href="/wiki/Negative_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Negative numbers">Negative numbers</a> are accepted as numeric by the late Han-era Chinese text <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nine_Chapters_on_the_Mathematical_Art" title="The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art">The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/Liu_Hui" title="Liu Hui">Liu Hui</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Cao Wei</a> (during the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period) writes down laws regarding the arithmetic of negative numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Hodgkin_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgkin-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> gives a new symbol for zero and uses it for the decimal system</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra_3">Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> discovers the formula for the square-pyramidal numbers (the sums of consecutive square numbers).<sup id="cite_ref-arbhsimp_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arbhsimp-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> discovers the formula for the simplicial numbers (the sums of consecutive cube numbers).<sup id="cite_ref-arbhsimp_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arbhsimp-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_discrete_mathematics_4">Number theory and discrete mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Number theory and discrete mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>3rd century AD: <a href="/wiki/Diophantus" title="Diophantus">Diophantus</a> discusses linear diophantine equations.</li>
<li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> discovers Bezout's identity, a foundational result to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Principal_ideal_domain" title="Principal ideal domain">principal ideal domains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Datta_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Datta-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> develops <a href="/wiki/Ku%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADaka" title="Kuṭṭaka">Kuṭṭaka</a>, an algorithm very similar to the <a href="/wiki/Extended_Euclidean_algorithm" title="Extended Euclidean algorithm">Extended Euclidean algorithm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Datta_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Datta-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry_5">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 60 AD: Heron's formula is discovered by <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria" title="Hero of Alexandria">Hero of Alexandria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 100 AD: <a href="/wiki/Menelaus_of_Alexandria" title="Menelaus of Alexandria">Menelaus of Alexandria</a> describes <a href="/wiki/Spherical_triangles" class="mw-redirect" title="Spherical triangles">spherical triangles</a>, a precursor to non-Euclidean geometry.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyer_Menelaus_of_Alexandria_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer_Menelaus_of_Alexandria-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>4th to 5th centuries: The modern fundamental trigonometric functions, sine and cosine, are described in the <a href="/wiki/Siddhanta" title="Siddhanta">Siddhantas</a> of India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_February_2021&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(February_2021)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_February_2021]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(February_2021)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> This formulation of trigonometry is an improvement over the earlier Greek functions, in that it lends itself more seamlessly to polar co-ordinates and the later complex interpretation of the trigonometric functions.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_mathematics_and_algorithms_3">Numerical mathematics and algorithms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Numerical mathematics and algorithms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>By the 4th century AD: a square root finding algorithm with quartic convergence, known as the <a href="/wiki/Methods_of_computing_square_roots#Bakhshali_method" title="Methods of computing square roots">Bakhshali method</a> (after the <a href="/wiki/Bakhshali_manuscript" title="Bakhshali manuscript">Bakhshali manuscript</a> which records it), is discovered in India.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> describes a numerical algorithm for finding cube roots.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> develops an algorithm to solve the Chinese remainder theorem.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKak1986_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKak1986-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1st to 4th century AD: A precursor to long division, known as "<a href="/wiki/Galley_division" title="Galley division">galley division</a>" is developed at some point. Its discovery is generally believed to have originated in India around the 4th century AD,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> although Singaporean mathematician <a href="/wiki/Lam_Lay_Yong" title="Lam Lay Yong">Lam Lay Yong</a> claims that the method is found in the Chinese text <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nine_Chapters_on_the_Mathematical_Art" title="The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art">The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art</a></i>, from the 1st century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Notation_and_conventions_2">Notation and conventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Notation and conventions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diophantus-cover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Diophantus-cover.jpg/170px-Diophantus-cover.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Diophantus-cover.jpg/255px-Diophantus-cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Diophantus-cover.jpg/340px-Diophantus-cover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="591" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diophantus-cover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diophantus' <i>Arithmetica</i> (pictured: a Latin translation from 1621) contained the first known use of symbolic mathematical notation. Despite the relative decline in the importance of the sciences during the Roman era, several Greek mathematicians continued to flourish in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>c. 150 AD: The <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> contains evidence of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_numerals#Zero" title="Greek numerals">Hellenistic zero</a>. Unlike the earlier Babylonian zero, the Hellenistic zero could be used alone, or at the end of a number. However, it was usually used in the fractional part of a numeral, and was not regarded as a true arithmetical number itself.</li>
<li>3rd century AD: <a href="/wiki/Diophantus" title="Diophantus">Diophantus</a> uses a primitive form of algebraic symbolism, which is quickly forgotten.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>By the 4th century AD: The present <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a> with <a href="/wiki/Positional_notation" title="Positional notation">place-value</a> numerals develops in <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta-era</a> India, and is attested in the <a href="/wiki/Bakhshali_Manuscript" class="mw-redirect" title="Bakhshali Manuscript">Bakhshali Manuscript</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara#Timeline" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The superiority of the system over existing place-value and sign-value systems arises from its treatment of <a href="/wiki/Zero" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero">zero</a> as an ordinary numeral.</li>
<li>By the 5th century AD: The decimal separator is developed in India,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> as recorded in <a href="/wiki/Al-Uqlidisi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Uqlidisi">al-Uqlidisi</a>'s later commentary on Indian mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-Berggren_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berggren-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>By 499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>'s work shows the use of the modern fraction notation, known as bhinnarasi.<sup id="cite_ref-jeff_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jeff-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics_2">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy_3">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 150 AD: Ptolemy's <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a> contains practical formulae to calculate latitudes and day lengths.</li>
<li>2nd century AD: <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> formalises the epicycles of Apollonius.</li>
<li>By the 5th century AD: The elliptical orbits of planets are discovered in India by at least the time of Aryabhata, and are used for the calculations of orbital periods and eclipse timings.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>499 AD: Historians speculate that <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> may have used an underlying heliocentric model for his astronomical calculations, which would make it the first computational heliocentric model in history (as opposed to Aristarchus's model in form).<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> This claim is based on his description of the planetary period about the sun (<i>śīghrocca</i>), but has been met with criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics_2">Optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>2nd century - <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> publishes his <a href="/wiki/Optics_(Ptolemy)" title="Optics (Ptolemy)">Optics</a>, discussing colour, reflection, and refraction of light, and including the first known table of refractive angles.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_and_human_anatomy">Biology and human anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Biology and human anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>2nd century AD: <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> studies the anatomy of pigs.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_and_geospatial_measurements_2">Astronomical and geospatial measurements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Astronomical and geospatial measurements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>499 AD: <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> creates a particularly accurate eclipse chart. As an example of its accuracy, 18th century scientist <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Le_Gentil" title="Guillaume Le Gentil">Guillaume Le Gentil</a>, during a visit to Pondicherry, India, found the Indian computations (based on Aryabhata's computational paradigm) of the duration of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse</a> of 30 August 1765 to be short by 41 seconds, whereas his charts (by Tobias Mayer, 1752) were long by 68 seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-Ansari_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ansari-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span id="500_AD_.E2.80.93_1000_AD"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="500_AD_–_1000_AD">500 AD – 1000 AD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: 500 AD – 1000 AD">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg/220px-Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg/330px-Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg/440px-Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1639" data-file-height="1852" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Indian_Rashtrakuta_Empire_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The age of Imperial Karnataka was a period of significant advancement in Indian mathematics.</div></div></div>
<p>The Golden Age of Indian mathematics and astronomy continues after the end of the Gupta empire, especially in Southern India during the era of the <a href="/wiki/Rashtrakuta_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Rashtrakuta Empire">Rashtrakuta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Chalukya_Empire" title="Western Chalukya Empire">Western Chalukya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vijayanagara_Empire" title="Vijayanagara Empire">Vijayanagara</a> empires of <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, which variously patronised Hindu and Jain mathematicians. In addition, the Middle East enters the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> through contact with other civilisations, and China enters a golden period during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> dynasties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_5">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Numbers.2C_measurement_and_arithmetic_4"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers,_measurement_and_arithmetic_4">Numbers, measurement and arithmetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Numbers, measurement and arithmetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>628 AD: <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> states the arithmetic rules for addition, subtraction, and multiplication with zero, as well as the multiplication of negative numbers, extending the basic rules for the latter found in the earlier <a href="/wiki/The_Nine_Chapters_on_the_Mathematical_Art" title="The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art">The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra_4">Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>628 AD: <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> provides an explicit solution to the <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_equation" title="Quadratic equation">quadratic equation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlofker2007428–434_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlofker2007428–434-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>9th century AD: Jain mathematician <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%ABra_(mathematician)" title="Mahāvīra (mathematician)">Mahāvīra</a> writes down a factorisation for the difference of cubes.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_discrete_mathematics_5">Number theory and discrete mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Number theory and discrete mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>628 AD: <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> writes down <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta%27s_identity" title="Brahmagupta&#39;s identity">Brahmagupta's identity</a>, an important lemma in the theory of <a href="/wiki/Pell%27s_equation" title="Pell&#39;s equation">Pell's equation</a>.</li>
<li>628 AD: <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> produces an infinite (but not exhaustive) number of solutions to <a href="/wiki/Pell%27s_equation" title="Pell&#39;s equation">Pell's equation</a>.</li>
<li>c. 850 AD: <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%ABra_(mathematician)" title="Mahāvīra (mathematician)">Mahāvīra</a> derives the expression for the binomial coefficient in terms of factorials, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle {\tbinom {n}{r}}={\tfrac {n!}{r!(n-r)!}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

          <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\tbinom {n}{r}}={\tfrac {n!}{r!(n-r)!}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1351fdfd73b4b5ce90f0a5129f162a23a5854d16" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:12.993ex; height:4.343ex;" alt="{\tbinom {n}{r}}={\tfrac {n!}{r!(n-r)!}}"/></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-ed-cam_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ed-cam-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 975 AD: <a href="/wiki/Halayudha" title="Halayudha">Halayudha</a> organizes the binomial coefficients into a triangle, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_triangle" title="Pascal&#39;s triangle">Pascal's triangle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ed-cam_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ed-cam-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry_6">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>628 AD: <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> discovers <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta%27s_formula" title="Brahmagupta&#39;s formula">Brahmagupta's formula</a>, a generalization of Heron's formula to cyclic quadrilaterals.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_2">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>10th century AD: Manjula in India discovers the derivative, deducing that the derivative of the sine function is the cosine.<sup id="cite_ref-pcock_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcock-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_mathematics_and_algorithms_4">Numerical mathematics and algorithms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Numerical mathematics and algorithms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>628 AD: <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> discovers second-order interpolation, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta%27s_interpolation_formula" title="Brahmagupta&#39;s interpolation formula">Brahmagupta's interpolation formula</a>.</li>
<li>629 AD: <a href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_I" title="Bhāskara I">Bhāskara I</a> produces the first approximation of a transcendental function with a rational function, in the <a href="/wiki/Bhaskara_I%27s_sine_approximation_formula" title="Bhaskara I&#39;s sine approximation formula">sine approximation formula</a> that bears his name.</li>
<li>816 AD: Jain mathematician <a href="/wiki/Virasena" title="Virasena">Virasena</a> describes the integer logarithm.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>9th century AD: <a href="/wiki/Algorism" title="Algorism">Algorisms</a> (arithmetical algorithms on numbers written in place-value system) are described by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi" title="Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi">al-Khwarizmi</a> in his <i>kitāb al-ḥisāb al-hindī</i> (<i>Book of Indian computation</i>) and <i>kitab al-jam' wa'l-tafriq al-ḥisāb al-hindī</i> (<i>Addition and subtraction in Indian arithmetic</i>).</li>
<li>9th century AD: <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%ABra_(mathematician)" title="Mahāvīra (mathematician)">Mahāvīra</a> discovers the first algorithm for writing fractions as Egyptian fractions,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKusuba2004497–516_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKusuba2004497–516-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> which is in fact a slightly more general form of the <a href="/wiki/Greedy_algorithm_for_Egyptian_fractions" title="Greedy algorithm for Egyptian fractions">Greedy algorithm for Egyptian fractions</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Notation_and_conventions_3">Notation and conventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Notation and conventions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>628 AD: <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> invents a symbolic mathematical notation, which is then adopted by mathematicians through India and the Near East, and eventually Europe.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics_3">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy_4">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>6th century AD: <a href="/wiki/Varahamira" class="mw-redirect" title="Varahamira">Varahamira</a> in the Gupta empire is the first to describe comets as astronomical phenomena, and as periodic in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-comets_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-comets-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanics_2">Mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 525 AD: <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> in Byzantine Egypt describes the notion of inertia, and states that the motion of a falling object does not depend on its weight.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> His radical rejection of Aristotlean orthodoxy lead him to be ignored in his time.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics_3">Optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>984 AD: <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Sahl_(mathematician)" title="Ibn Sahl (mathematician)">Ibn Sahl</a> discovers <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell&#39;s law">Snell's law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_and_geospatial_measurements_3">Astronomical and geospatial measurements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Astronomical and geospatial measurements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>10th century AD: Kashmiri<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> astronomer <a href="/wiki/Bha%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADotpala" class="mw-redirect" title="Bhaṭṭotpala">Bhaṭṭotpala</a> lists names and estimates periods of certain comets.<sup id="cite_ref-comets_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-comets-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span id="1000_AD_.E2.80.93_1500_AD"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1000_AD_–_1500_AD">1000 AD – 1500 AD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: 1000 AD – 1500 AD">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_6">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra_5">Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>11th century: <a href="/wiki/Alhazen" class="mw-redirect" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a> discovers the formula for the simplicial numbers defined as the sums of consecutive quartic powers.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_discrete_mathematics_6">Number theory and discrete mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Number theory and discrete mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>c. 1000 AD: <a href="/wiki/Al-Karaji" title="Al-Karaji">al-Karaji</a> uses mathematical induction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz1998255_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz1998255-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>12th century AD: <a href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_II" title="Bhāskara II">Bhāskara II</a> develops the <a href="/wiki/Chakravala_method" title="Chakravala method">Chakravala method</a>, solving Pell's equation.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry_7">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>15th century: <a href="/wiki/Parameshvara" class="mw-redirect" title="Parameshvara">Parameshvara</a> discovers a formula for the circumradius of a quadrilateral.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_3">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>1380 AD: <a href="/wiki/Madhava_of_Sangamagrama" title="Madhava of Sangamagrama">Madhava of Sangamagrama</a> develops the <a href="/wiki/Taylor_series" title="Taylor series">Taylor series</a>, and derives the Taylor series representation for the sine, cosine and arctangent functions, and uses it to produce the <a href="/wiki/Leibniz_formula_for_%CF%80" title="Leibniz formula for π">Leibniz series for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz1995_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz1995-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1380 AD: <a href="/wiki/Madhava_of_Sangamagrama" title="Madhava of Sangamagrama">Madhava of Sangamagrama</a> discusses error terms in infinite series in the context of his infinite series for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-mact-biog_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mact-biog-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1380 AD: <a href="/wiki/Madhava_of_Sangamagrama" title="Madhava of Sangamagrama">Madhava of Sangamagrama</a> discovers <a href="/wiki/Continued_fractions" class="mw-redirect" title="Continued fractions">continued fractions</a> and uses them to solve transcendental equations.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearce_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1380 AD: The Kerala school develops convergence tests for infinite series.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz1995_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz1995-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 1500 AD: <a href="/wiki/Nilakantha_Somayaji" title="Nilakantha Somayaji">Nilakantha Somayaji</a> discovers an infinite series for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoy1990101–102_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoy1990101–102-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_mathematics_and_algorithms_5">Numerical mathematics and algorithms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Timeline_of_scientific_discoveries&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Numerical mathematics and algorithms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>12th century AD: <a href="/wiki/Sharaf_al-D%C4%ABn_al-%E1%B9%AC%C5%ABs%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharaf al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī">al-Tusi</a> develops a numerical algorithm to solve cubic equations.</li>
<li>1380 AD: <a href="/wiki/Madhava_of_Sangamagrama" title="Madhava of Sangamagrama">Madhava of Sangamagrama</a> solves transcendental equations by iteration.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearce_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></li>
